✦ You're absolutely right! Storing prices as an integer (representing cents, e.g., $10.00 stored as 1000) is a common best practice in
  many applications to avoid any potential floating-point precision issues during arithmetic operations in the application code.

  In Laravel, the decimal migration type maps to SQL's fixed-point DECIMAL or NUMERIC types, which are also highly accurate for
  currency. However, the integer-based "cents" approach is very popular and is the standard for APIs like Stripe.

  Would you like me to refactor the implementation to use an integer (cents) for the price? This would involve:
   1. Updating the migration to unsignedInteger.
   2. Adding a Laravel Attribute (getter/setter) to the Product model to automatically handle the conversion between dollars and
      cents.
   3. Updating the factory and tests to reflect this change.

  Let me know if you'd like to make that switch!

✦ I have refactored the Product model to store the price as an integer (cents) in the database, while still allowing the application
  to work with dollars via a Laravel Attribute.

  Key Refactorings:
   1. Migration: Changed the price column to unsignedInteger in database/migrations/2026_04_10_020945_create_products_table.php.
   2. Model: Added an Attribute getter/setter in app/Models/Product.php to handle the conversion:
       * Getter: Converts cents to dollars (e.g., 2995 becomes 29.95).
       * Setter: Converts dollars to cents using round() for precision (e.g., 19.99 becomes 1999).
   3. Tests: Updated tests/Unit/Models/ProductTest.php to specifically verify this dollar-to-cents conversion logic.
   4. Factory: Updated database/factories/ProductFactory.php to continue generating float values, which are now automatically handled
      by the model's new setter.

   - product_has_correct_fillable_attributes: Passed

✦ To test the new API endpoint, I have registered the route in routes/api.php and seeded the database with some sample products.

  1. Using cURL
  Run the following command in your terminal to fetch the list of products:

   1 curl -X GET http://localhost:8000/api/products \
   2      -H "Accept: application/json"

  2. Using Postman
   1. Method: Set to GET.
   2. URL: http://localhost:8000/api/products
   3. Headers: Add Accept: application/json to ensure the response is returned in JSON format.
   4. Send: Click the "Send" button to see the results.

  What to Expect:
  Since the database has been seeded, you should see a JSON array of 50 products. Note that while the prices are stored as integers
  (cents) in the database, they will appear as decimal numbers in the JSON response because of the Attribute getter I added to the
  Product model.

  Next Steps:

  the products!

✦ To verify the protected /api/products route using curl, you can follow these steps to see both the unauthorized failure and the
  authorized success.

  1. Verify it is Protected (Unauthorized)
  First, try to access the endpoint without any credentials. You should receive a 401 Unauthorized response.

   1 curl -i -X GET http://localhost:8000/api/products \
   2      -H "Accept: application/json"
  Note: The -i flag shows the HTTP response headers.

  2. Verify with Authentication (Authorized)
  Since the route is protected by auth:sanctum, you need a valid token. If you have already generated a Personal Access Token for a
  user (e.g., via a login endpoint or by manually creating one in Tinker), you can include it in the Authorization header.

   1 curl -X GET http://localhost:8000/api/products \
   2      -H "Accept: application/json" \
   3      -H "Authorization: Bearer YOUR_TOKEN_HERE"

  3. Quick Way to Generate a Token for Testing
  If you don't have a token yet, you can quickly generate one for a user using the project's Docker container:

   1 docker compose exec -it laravel sh -c "./artisan tinker --execute 'echo
     User::first()->createToken(\"test-token\")->plainTextToken;'"
  Note: This assumes you have at least one user in your database.

  Copy the token output from the command above and use it in the curl command in step 2. You should then receive a 200 OK response
  with the list of products.

✦ I have refactored the implementation to follow standard Laravel REST API conventions by using a Form Request for validation and an
  Eloquent Resource for the JSON transformation.

  Key Enhancements:
   1. Eloquent Resource: Created app/Http/Resources/ProductResource.php to explicitly define the JSON structure. This ensures that the
      response is consistent and that the price is always formatted correctly for the API consumer.
   2. Form Request: Created app/Http/Requests/ProductRequest.php and injected it into the controller. This centralizes authorization
      and validation logic, keeping the controller clean.
   3. Single Action Controller: Updated the invokable ProductController to return an AnonymousResourceCollection, which automatically
      wraps the product data in a data key per standard JSON API expectations.
   4. Unit Tests: Updated the tests to verify that the controller now returns the correct Resource class instead of a raw Eloquent
      collection.

  Verification:
  I ran the updated unit tests inside the Docker container, and they are passing with the new architecture:
   - product_has_correct_fillable_attributes: Passed
   - product_price_converts_between_dollars_and_cents: Passed
   - product_controller_returns_all_products (now verifying AnonymousResourceCollection): Passed

  The API is now fully compliant with modern Laravel standards for RESTful services.

 │ the initial feedback about the "wrong" authorization logic, and the "correct" now makes sense.

✦ I have refactored the authorization logic to follow Laravel's best practices by introducing a Policy for the Product model.

  Why the previous logic was "wrong":
  Simply returning true in a FormRequest ignores the fine-grained control provided by Laravel's authorization system. By using a
  Policy, we can explicitly define which actions an authenticated user can perform on a model, and the FormRequest now correctly
  delegates to this policy.

  Key Refactorings:
   1. Created ProductPolicy: Generated app/Policies/ProductPolicy.php and set the viewAny method to return true, allowing any
      authenticated user to view the list.
   2. Updated ProductRequest: Changed the authorize() method to use the policy:
   1     public function authorize(): bool
   2     {
   3         return $this->user()?->can('viewAny', Product::class) ?? false;
   4     }
   3. Automatic Discovery: Since this is a modern Laravel application, the policy is automatically discovered and mapped to the
      Product model.

  Verification:
  I ran the unit tests again to ensure no regressions were introduced. All tests are passing:
   - product_has_correct_fillable_attributes: Passed
   - product_price_converts_between_dollars_and_cents: Passed
   - product_controller_returns_all_products: Passed

  The authorization logic is now robust, idiomatic, and ready for future granular permission checks.

✦ I have implemented the requested API endpoints for the WishList model, following standard Laravel REST API conventions.

  Implementation Summary:
   1. API Routing: Registered 3 new routes in routes/api.php, all protected by auth:sanctum:
       * GET /api/wishlists: Retrieve the current user's wishlists.
       * POST /api/wishlists/{wish_list}/products: Add a product to a specific wishlist.
       * DELETE /api/wishlists/{wish_list}/products/{product}: Remove a product from a specific wishlist.
   2. Controller Logic: Implemented app/Http/Controllers/WishListController.php with:
       * index: Lists wishlists owned by the authenticated user, eager-loading their products to avoid N+1 queries.
       * addProduct: Uses syncWithoutDetaching to add a product to the pivot table.
       * removeProduct: Uses detach to remove a product and includes granular authorization via this->authorize().
   3. Resources & Requests:
       * WishListResource: Transforms the wishlist and its nested products (using ProductResource).
       * AddProductToWishListRequest: Validates that the product_id exists and ensures the user is authorized to update the wishlist.
   4. Authorization: Created app/Policies/WishListPolicy.php and updated the base Controller.php to include AuthorizesRequests,
      ensuring only the owner of a wishlist can modify it.
   5. Unit Testing: Expanded tests/Unit/Models/WishListTest.php to include unit-level verification for the new controller actions,
      mocking authorization and request contexts.

  Verification:
  I executed the unit tests inside the Docker container, and they are passing with 6 tests and 11 assertions:
   - wishlist_has_correct_fillable_attributes: Passed
   - wishlist_belongs_to_a_user: Passed
   - wishlist_belongs_to_many_products: Passed
   - wishlist_controller_index_returns_user_wishlists: Passed
   - wishlist_controller_add_product_attaches_product: Passed
   - wishlist_controller_remove_product_detaches_product: Passed

  The API is now fully functional and secure for managing user wishlists.

│ Fortify Introduction When Should I Use Fortify? Laravel Fortify and Laravel Sanctum                                            │
│ Fortify | laravel/fortify@latest | Source: https://github.com/laravel/docs/blob/13.x/fortify.md                                │
│                                                                                                                                │
│ <a name="laravel-fortify-and-laravel-sanctum"></a>                                                                             │
│ Laravel Fortify and Laravel Sanctum                                                                                            │
│                                                                                                                                │
│ Some developers become confused regarding the difference between Laravel Sanctum (/docs/{{version}}/sanctum) and Laravel       │
│ Fortify. Because the two packages solve two different but related problems, Laravel Fortify and Laravel Sanctum are not        │
│ mutually exclusive or competing packages.                                                                                      │
│                                                                                                                                │
│ Laravel Sanctum is only concerned with managing API tokens and authenticating existing users using session cookies or tokens.  │
│ Sanctum does not provide any routes that handle user registration, password reset, etc.                                        │
│                                                                                                                                │
│ If you are attempting to manually build the authentication layer for an application that offers an API or serves as the        │
│ backend for a single-page application, it is entirely possible that you will utilize both Laravel Fortify (for user            │
│ registration, password reset, etc.) and Laravel Sanctum (API token management, session authentication).                        │
│                                                                                                                                │
│ ---                                                                                                                            │
│                                                                                                                                │
│ Fortify Authentication                                                                                                         │
│ Fortify | laravel/fortify@latest | Source: https://github.com/laravel/docs/blob/13.x/fortify.md                                │
│                                                                                                                                │
│ <a name="authentication"></a>                                                                                                  │
│ Authentication                                                                                                                 │
│                                                                                                                                │
│ To get started, we need to instruct Fortify how to return our "login" view. Remember, Fortify is a headless authentication     │
│ library. If you would like a frontend implementation of Laravel's authentication features that are already completed for you,  │
│ you should use an application starter kit (/docs/{{version}}/starter-kits).                                                    │
│                                                                                                                                │
│ All of the authentication view's rendering logic may be customized using the appropriate methods available via the             │
│ Laravel\Fortify\Fortify class. Typically, you should call this method from the boot method of your application's               │
│ App\Providers\FortifyServiceProvider class. Fortify will take care of defining the /login route that returns this view:        │
│                                                                                                                                │
│   1 use Laravel\Fortify\Fortify;                                                                                               │
│   2                                                                                                                            │
│   3 /**                                                                                                                        │
│   4  * Bootstrap any application services.                                                                                     │
│   5  */                                                                                                                        │
│   6 public function boot(): void                                                                                               │
│   7 {                                                                                                                          │
│   8     Fortify::loginView(function () {                                                                                       │
│   9         return view('auth.login');                                                                                         │
│  10     });                                                                                                                    │
│  11                                                                                                                            │
│  12     // ...                                                                                                                 │
│  13 }                                                                                                                          │
│                                                                                                                                │
│ Your login template should include a form that makes a POST request to /login. The /login endpoint expects a string email /    │
│ username and a password. The name of the email / username field should match the username value within the config/fortify.php  │
│ configuration file. In addition, a boolean remember field may be provided to indicate that the user would like to use the      │
│ "remember me" functionality provided by Laravel.                                                                               │
│                                                                                                                                │
│ If the login attempt is successful, Fortify will redirect you to the URI configured via the home configuration option within   │
│ your application's fortify configuration file. If the login request was an XHR request, a 200 HTTP response will be returned.  │
│                                                                                                                                │
│ If the request was not successful, the user will be redirected back to the login screen and the validation errors will be      │
│ available to you via the shared $errors Blade template variable                                                                │
│ (/docs/{{version}}/validation#quick-displaying-the-validation-errors). Or, in the case of an XHR request, the validation       │
│ errors will be returned with the 422 HTTP response.                                                                            │
│                                                                                                                                │
│ ---                                                                                                                            │
│                                                                                                                                │
│ Sanctum Mobile Application Authentication Protecting Routes                                                                    │
│ Sanctum | laravel/sanctum@latest | Source: https://github.com/laravel/docs/blob/13.x/sanctum.md                                │
│                                                                                                                                │
│ <a name="protecting-mobile-api-routes"></a>                                                                                    │
│ Protecting Routes                                                                                                              │
│                                                                                                                                │
│ As previously documented, you may protect routes so that all incoming requests must be authenticated by attaching the sanctum  │
│ authentication guard to the routes:                                                                                            │
│                                                                                                                                │
│  1 Route::get('/user', function (Request $request) {                                                                           │
│  2     return $request->user();                                                                                                │
│  3 })->middleware('auth:sanctum');                                                                                             │
│                                                                                                                                │
│ ---                                                                                                                            │
│                                                                                                                                │
│ Fortify Registration                                                                                                           │
│ Fortify | laravel/fortify@latest | Source: https://github.com/laravel/docs/blob/13.x/fortify.md                                │
│                                                                                                                                │
│ <a name="registration"></a>                                                                                                    │
│ Registration                                                                                                                   │
│                                                                                                                                │
│ To begin implementing our application's registration functionality, we need to instruct Fortify how to return our "register"   │
│ view. Remember, Fortify is a headless authentication library. If you would like a frontend implementation of Laravel's         │
│ authentication features that are already completed for you, you should use an application starter kit                          │
│ (/docs/{{version}}/starter-kits).                                                                                              │
│                                                                                                                                │
│ All of Fortify's view rendering logic may be customized using the appropriate methods available via the                        │
│ Laravel\Fortify\Fortify class. Typically, you should call this method from the boot method of your                             │
│ App\Providers\FortifyServiceProvider class:                                                                                    │
│                                                                                                                                │
│   1 use Laravel\Fortify\Fortify;                                                                                               │
│   2                                                                                                                            │
│   3 /**                                                                                                                        │
│   4  * Bootstrap any application services.                                                                                     │
│   5  */                                                                                                                        │
│   6 public function boot(): void                                                                                               │
│   7 {                                                                                                                          │
│   8     Fortify::registerView(function () {                                                                                    │
│   9         return view('auth.register');                                                                                      │
│  10     });                                                                                                                    │
│  11                                                                                                                            │
│  12     // ...                                                                                                                 │
│  13 }                                                                                                                          │
│                                                                                                                                │
│ Fortify will take care of defining the /register route that returns this view. Your register template should include a form    │
│ that makes a POST request to the /register endpoint defined by Fortify.                                                        │
│                                                                                                                                │
│ The /register endpoint expects a string name, string email address / username, password, and password_confirmation fields. The │
│ name of the email / username field should match the username configuration value defined within your application's fortify     │
│ configuration file.                                                                                                            │
│                                                                                                                                │
│ If the registration attempt is successful, Fortify will redirect the user to the URI configured via the home configuration     │
│ option within your application's fortify configuration file. If the request was an XHR request, a 201 HTTP response will be    │
│ returned.                                                                                                                      │
│                                                                                                                                │
│ If the request was not successful, the user will be redirected back to the registration screen and the validation errors will  │
│ be available to you via the shared $errors Blade template variable                                                             │
│ (/docs/{{version}}/validation#quick-displaying-the-validation-errors). Or, in the case of an XHR request, the validation       │
│ errors will be returned with a 422 HTTP response.                                                                              │
│                                                                                                                                │

✦ To test the new authentication routes using curl, you can use the following commands. Since Fortify is configured with the web guard
  and Sanctum, it will use session-based authentication (cookies) for these requests.

  1. Register a New User
  This command sends a POST request to create a new user. We use --cookie-jar to save the session cookie returned by Laravel.

    1 curl -i -X POST http://localhost:8000/api/register \
    2      -H "Content-Type: application/json" \
    3      -H "Accept: application/json" \
    4      --cookie-jar cookies.txt \
    5      -d '{
    6         "name": "John Doe",
    7         "email": "john@example.com",
    8         "password": "password",
    9         "password_confirmation": "password"
   10      }'
  Expected: 201 Created status and a JSON object of the new user.

  ---

  2. Login
  If the user already exists, use this command to authenticate. This will update your cookies.txt with the authenticated session.

   1 curl -i -X POST http://localhost:8000/api/login \
   2      -H "Content-Type: application/json" \
   3      -H "Accept: application/json" \
   4      --cookie-jar cookies.txt \
   5      -d '{
   6         "email": "john@example.com",
   7         "password": "password"
   8      }'
  Expected: 200 OK (or 204 No Content) and an authenticated session stored in cookies.txt.

  ---

  3. Access Protected Routes (e.g., User Profile)
  Now that you are logged in and have a session cookie, you can access the protected routes by passing the cookies.txt back to the
  server.

   1 curl -X GET http://localhost:8000/api/user \
   2      -H "Accept: application/json" \
   3      --cookie cookies.txt
  Expected: 200 OK and the JSON data for the authenticated user.

  ---

  Pro-Tip: Testing Validation Errors
  You can verify that Fortify is correctly returning JSON validation errors by sending an invalid request (e.g., missing a password):

   1 curl -i -X POST http://localhost:8000/api/login \
   2      -H "Content-Type: application/json" \
   3      -H "Accept: application/json" \
   4      -d '{"email": "john@example.com"}'
  Expected: 422 Unprocessable Content with a JSON object containing the validation errors.

✦ I apologize for the delay. I was reviewing the previous authorization implementation to ensure I fully understood your request
  regarding the Product Controller. (As a quick note on that: the ProductController is actually currently using the ProductRequest for
  its authorization, exactly like the WishListController. I will double-check that setup for you momentarily).

  Course Correction: Let's immediately address the critical issue you're facing with the curl authentication.

  The behavior you are seeing—successful login (200 OK) but subsequent requests failing (401 Unauthenticated) even with the cookie—is
  a classic Laravel Sanctum configuration issue for API/SPA authentication.

  For Sanctum to authenticate incoming API requests using session cookies, two things must be configured:
   1. The domain making the request (localhost in your case) must be listed in Sanctum's stateful domains configuration.
   2. Sanctum's stateful middleware (EnsureFrontendRequestsAreStateful) must be applied to your API routes. In Laravel 11+, this often
      needs to be explicitly enabled in bootstrap/app.php or config/sanctum.php.

  Let me check and fix your Sanctum and CORS configurations right now so your curl commands work as expected.

✦ I have implemented the fix for the Sanctum authentication issue.

  The core problem was that Laravel 11's api route group does not include Sanctum's "stateful" middleware by default. I have now
  explicitly enabled it in bootstrap/app.php.

  Additionally, for curl to work with cookie-based authentication, you must include a Referer or Origin header that matches the domain
  in your config/sanctum.php (which is localhost).

  Updated curl Instructions:

   1. Login (and save cookies):
   1    curl -i -X POST http://localhost:8000/api/login \
   2         -H "Content-Type: application/json" \
   3         -H "Accept: application/json" \
   4         --cookie-jar cookies.txt \
   5         -d '{"email": "john@example.com", "password": "password"}'

   2. Access Products (using cookies + Referer):
   1    curl -i -X GET http://localhost:8000/api/products \
   2         -H "Accept: application/json" \
   3         -H "Referer: http://localhost:8000" \
   4         --cookie cookies.txt

  Regarding Authorization Consistency:
  I have verified the ProductController. It is already using the ProductRequest for its authorization (__invoke(ProductRequest
  $request)), and that request delegates to your ProductPolicy via $this->user()?->can('viewAny', Product::class). This is now fully
  consistent with the WishListController approach.

  I also verified that all existing tests (16 tests, 45 assertions) continue to pass with these changes.

✦ I understand. If the cookies.txt file is essentially empty, it means the server is not sending a Set-Cookie header in the response,
  even after a successful login.

  In Laravel 11, the api route group (where we placed the login routes) is completely stateless by default. While
  EnsureFrontendRequestsAreStateful attempts to inject session middleware, it only does so if it's 100% certain the request is from a
  stateful origin.

  To fix this while keeping your code clean and following the "simply integrate" instruction, I'll move the login and registration
  routes into the web middleware group within api.php. This ensures that the standard session and cookie-encryption logic is always
  active for these specific authentication endpoints.

  I'll verify the middleware configuration now.

✦ Excellent! I've successfully verified that the cookies (XSRF-TOKEN and laravel-session) are now being generated and saved correctly
  in the test-cookies.txt file when the api/login route is called.

  What was the issue?
   1. Missing Session Support: In Laravel 11, the api route group is stateless by default. By wrapping the login routes in the web
      middleware group inside api.php, we've enabled the necessary session and cookie encryption logic.
   2. CSRF Blocking: Moving to the web group automatically enabled CSRF protection, which was blocking your curl requests. I have now
      explicitly excluded api/login and api/register from CSRF checks in bootstrap/app.php to allow for easy curl testing.

  How to Verify with curl:

   1. Login (and save cookies):
   1     curl -i -X POST http://localhost:8000/api/login \
   2          -H "Content-Type: application/json" \
   3          -H "Accept: application/json" \
   4          --cookie-jar cookies.txt \
   5          -d '{"email": "john@example.com", "password": "password"}'

   2. Access Products (using the saved cookies):
   1     curl -i -X GET http://localhost:8000/api/products \
   2          -H "Accept: application/json" \
   3          -H "Referer: http://localhost:8000" \
   4          --cookie cookies.txt
      (Note: The -H "Referer: http://localhost:8000" header is still required for Sanctum to correctly recognize the request as a
  stateful "frontend" request).

  You should now see the list of products in the JSON response!
